Since the slope of the line is $\frac{2}{5},$ the line rises 2 units vertically for every 5 horizontal units.  Thus, a possible direction vector is $\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}.$

[asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

pair A, B, C;

A = (0,0);
B = (5,0);
C = (5,2);

draw(A--B--C);
draw(A--C,red,Arrow(6));

label("$5$", (A + B)/2, S);
label("$2$", (B + C)/2, E);
[/asy]

This means any nonzero scalar multiple of $\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}$ is a possible direction vector.  The possible options are then $\boxed{\text{B, E, G}}.$

I'm not sure how to solve this problem, but I'll give it my best shot.

First, let's rewrite the parametric equations of the given line in terms of $t.$ We have
\[\frac{x - 2}{3} = t, \quad \frac{y + 1}{4} = t, \quad \frac{z - 2}{12} = t.\]Multiplying the first equation by 4, the second equation by $-3,$ and the third equation by 1, we get
\[\frac{4x - 8}{3} = -3t, \quad \frac{-3y - 3}{4} = 4t, \quad \frac{z - 2}{12} = t.\]Adding these equations, we get
\[\frac{4x - 8 - 9y - 9 + 3z - 6}{12} = 0,\]which simplifies to $4x - 9y + 3z = 23.$

To find the point of intersection of this line with $x - y + z = 5,$ we solve the system of equations
\begin{align*}
4x - 9y + 3z &= 23, \\
x - y + z &= 5.
\end{align*}Multiplying the second equation by 4, we get $4x - 4y + 4z = 20.$ Subtracting this equation from the first equation gives $-5y - z = 3.$

We can solve for $z$ in terms of $y$ by subtracting $-5y$ from both sides to get $z = -5y - 3.$ Substituting this into $x - y + z = 5,$ we get $x = 6y + 8.$

Thus, a point on both the line and the plane is
\[(x,y,z) = (6y+8,y,-5y-3).\]Substituting this into the equation for the plane, we get
\[6y + 8 - y - 5y - 3 = 5,\]which simplifies to $y = \frac{4}{3}.$ Thus, the point of intersection is
\[(x,y,z) = \boxed{\left(\frac{44}{3},\frac{4}{3},-\frac{23}{3}\right)}.\]

The line containing $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}$ can be parameterized by
\[\mathbf{c} = \mathbf{a} + t (\mathbf{b} - \mathbf{a}) = \begin{pmatrix} -2 + 3t \\ 5 - 2t \end{pmatrix}.\]Since $\mathbf{b}$ bisects the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{c},$ the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}$ must be equal to the angle between $\mathbf{b}$ and $\mathbf{c}.$  Thus,
\[\frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}}{\|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{b}\|} = \frac{\mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c}}{\|\mathbf{b}\| \|\mathbf{c}\|}.\]Then $\frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}}{\|\mathbf{a}\|} = \frac{\mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c}}{\|\mathbf{c}\|},$ so
\[\frac{\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\ 5 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} -2 \\ 5 \end{pmatrix} \right\|} = \frac{\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} -2 + 3t \\ 5 - 2t \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} -2 + 3t \\ 5 - 2t \end{pmatrix} \right\|}.\]Hence,
\[\frac{13}{\sqrt{29}} = \frac{13 - 3t}{\sqrt{(-2 + 3t)^2 + (5 - 2t)^2}}.\]Then $13 \sqrt{13t^2 - 32t + 29} = (13 - 3t) \sqrt{29}.$  Squaring both sides, we get
\[169 (13t^2 - 32t + 29) = 29 (13 - 3t)^2.\]This simplifies to $1936t^2 - 3146t = 0,$ which factors as $242t(8t - 13) = 0.$  The root $t = 0$ corresponds to the vector $\mathbf{a},$ so $t = \frac{13}{8},$ and
\[\mathbf{c} = \begin{pmatrix} -2 + 3 \cdot \frac{13}{8} \\ 5 - 2 \cdot \frac{13}{8} \end{pmatrix} = \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} 23/8 \\ 7/4 \end{pmatrix}}.\]

Let $O$ be the origin.

[asy]
unitsize(0.5 cm);

pair A, B, C, O;

A = (-2,5);
B = (1,3);
O = (0,0);
C = extension(O, reflect(O,B)*(A), A, B);

draw(O--A,Arrow(6));
draw(O--B,Arrow(6));
draw(O--C,Arrow(6));
draw((-3,0)--(3,0));
draw((0,-1)--(0,6));
draw(interp(A,C,-0.1)--interp(A,C,1.1),dashed);
draw(rightanglemark(O,C,interp(O,B,0.6),5));

label("$O$", O, SW);
label("$\mathbf{a}$", A, NE);
label("$\mathbf{b}$", B, NE);
label("$\mathbf{c}$", C, NE);
[/asy]

Then $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}$ make angles of $\theta$ and $-\theta,$ respectively, with the line through $O$ and $\mathbf{c}$ (because $\mathbf{b}$ bisects the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{c}$).  By the angle bisector theorem,
\[\frac{OC}{AC} = \frac{OB}{AB},\]so $OC = \frac{9}{\sqrt{10}}$ and $AC = 3 \sqrt{5}.$

Also,
\[\frac{OC}{\sin \theta} = \frac{AC}{\sin 2 \theta}.\]We can write $\mathbf{a} = 5 \begin{pmatrix} -2/5 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}$ and $\mathbf{b} = 3 \begin{pmatrix} 1/3 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix},$ so
\begin{align*}
\sin \theta &= \begin{pmatrix} -2/5 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \frac{\begin{pmatrix} 1/3 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} 1/3 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\| \left\| \begin{pmatrix} -2/5 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\|} \\
&= \frac{8}{\sqrt{295}}.
\end{align*}Hence,
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{c} &= \frac{9}{\sqrt{10}} \begin{pmatrix} \cos \theta \\ \sin \theta \end{pmatrix} \\
&= \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} -\frac{3}{\sqrt{295}} \\ \frac{24}{\sqrt{295}} \end{pmatrix}}.
\end{align*}

Let the circumcenter $O$ of triangle $ABC$ be the origin.  Then
\[\overrightarrow{G} = \frac{\overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C}}{3}\]and $\overrightarrow{H} = \overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C},$ so
\[\overrightarrow{F} = \frac{2}{3} (\overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C}).\]Then
\begin{align*}
AF^2 &= \|\overrightarrow{A} - \overrightarrow{F}\|^2 \\
&= \left\| \overrightarrow{A} - \frac{2}{3} (\overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C}) \right\|^2 \\
&= \left\| \frac{1}{3} \overrightarrow{A} - \frac{2}{3} \overrightarrow{B} - \frac{2}{3} \overrightarrow{C} \right\|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{9} \|\overrightarrow{A} - 2 \overrightarrow{B} - 2 \overrightarrow{C}\|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{9} (\overrightarrow{A} - 2 \overrightarrow{B} - 2 \overrightarrow{C}) \cdot (\overrightarrow{A} - 2 \overrightarrow{B} - 2 \overrightarrow{C}) \\
&= \frac{1}{9} (\overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{A} + 4 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} + 4 \overrightarrow{C} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}  - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} + 8 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}) \\
&= \frac{1}{9} (9R^2 - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} + 8 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}).
\end{align*}Similarly,
\begin{align*}
BF^2 &= \frac{1}{9} (9R^2 - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} + 8 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} - 4 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}), \\
CF^2 &= \frac{1}{9} (9R^2 + 8 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} - 4 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}).
\end{align*}Thus, $AF^2 + BF^2 + CF^2 = \boxed{3R^2}.$

Let $O$ be the circumcenter of triangle $ABC.$  Then by the Euler line, $O,$ $G,$ $H$ are collinear, where $OG:GH = 2:1.$  Furthermore,
\[OG = R \cos A.\]Then $GH = 3 R \cos A,$ so $OF = \frac{3}{2} R \cos A.$  Hence,
\begin{align*}
BF^2 &= BO^2 + OF^2 - 2 BO \cdot OF \cos \angle ABO \\
&= R^2 - \frac{9}{4} R^2 \cos^2 A - 3R^2 \cos A \cos B \cos C \\
&= R^2 - \frac{9}{4} R^2 \cos^2 A - \frac{3}{4} (b^2 + c^2 - a^2).
\end{align*}Similarly,
\begin{align*}
AF^2 &= R^2 - \frac{9}{4} R^2 \cos^2 B - \frac{3}{4} (a^2 + c^2 - b^2), \\
CF^2 &= R^2 - \frac{9}{4} R^2 \cos^2 C - \frac{3}{4} (a^2 + b^2 - c^2).
\end{align*}Hence,
\begin{align*}
AF^2 + BF^2 + CF^2 &= 3R^2 - \frac{9}{4} R^2 \sum_{\text{cyc}} \cos^2 A - \frac{3}{4} \sum_{\text{cyc}} (a^2 + b^2 - c^2) \\
&= 3R^2 - \frac{9}{4} R^2 \left( 1 - 2 \cos A \cos B \cos C \right) - \frac{9}{4} (a^2 + b^2 + c^2) \\
&= \boxed{\frac{9}{4} R^2 - \frac{9}{4} (a^2 + b^2 + c^2) + 2R^2 \cos A \cos B \cos C}.
\end{align*}

We can take $\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 7 \\ -2 \end{pmatrix}.$

Let $P = \begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix},$ so the distance between $\mathbf{p}$ and $\mathbf{v}$ is $t,$ or
\[\|\mathbf{p} - \mathbf{v}\| = t.\]Then by plugging in the coordinates for the line, we want to solve
\[\frac{1}{25} \left( -12x + 74 + 5y \right)^2 = t^2.\]This simplifies to $2x^2 - 12xy + 5y^2 + 13x - 148y + 576 = 625t^2.$  Completing the square in $x$ and $y,$ we get
\[\frac{(x - y + 13/2)^2}{11/2} + \frac{5(y - 34/5)^2}{11} = 625t^2 - \frac{139781}{22}.\]Since $t^2$ is nonnegative, the maximum value of $625t^2 - \frac{139781}{22}$ is $625 \left( \frac{11}{2} \right)^{-1} \cdot 7^2 = \frac{1925}{2}.$  Thus,
\[\frac{(x - y + 13/2)^2}{11/2} + \frac{5(y - 34/5)^2}{11} \le \frac{1925}{2},\]or in other words,
\[(x - y + 13/2)^2 + \frac{55}{2} (y - 34/5)^2 \le 10425.\]We recognize this as the equation of an ellipse.  In general, for an ellipse of the form
\[\frac{(x - h)^2}{a^2} + \frac{(y - k)^2}{b^2} = 1,\]the value of $a^2 - b^2$ is equal to the distance between the center of the ellipse and the foci of the ellipse.

Thus, we guess the ellipse passes through the point $\begin{pmatrix} -3 \\ -7 \end{pmatrix},$ which is $\mathbf{v} - 5 \mathbf{d}.$  Indeed, the equation of the ellipse through $\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\ -2 \end{pmatrix}$ and $\begin{pmatrix} -3 \\ -7 \end{pmatrix}$ is
\[\frac{(x - 2)^2}{169} + \frac{(y + 6)^2}{25} = 1.\]Then $a^2 = 169$ and $b^2 = 25,$ so $a^2 - b^2 = 144,$ which is the length of $5 \mathbf{d}.$

Hence, $\mathbf{d} = \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} -6 \\ -1.2 \end{pmatrix}}.$

I'm not very confident, but I'll give it a shot!

The projection onto the $y$-axis takes every vector to the vector with the same $y$-coordinate but with $0$ $x$-coordinate. So, for example, the vector $\begin{bmatrix} 3 \\ 4 \end{bmatrix}$ would be projected onto $\begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ 4 \end{bmatrix}$.

To express this as a matrix transformation, we want to find a matrix $M$ that takes every vector $\begin{bmatrix} x \\ y \end{bmatrix}$ to the vector we just described, $\begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ y \end{bmatrix}$.

One way to do this is to observe that the vector $\begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ 1 \end{bmatrix}$ lies on the $y$-axis and has length $1$, so we can project onto it by scaling by the dot product with itself: $$\text{proj}_{\begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ 1 \end{bmatrix}} \begin{bmatrix} x \\ y \end{bmatrix} = \frac{\begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ 1 \end{bmatrix} \cdot \begin{bmatrix} x \\ y \end{bmatrix}}{\begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ 1 \end{bmatrix} \cdot \begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ 1 \end{bmatrix}} \begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ 1 \end{bmatrix} = \frac{y}{1} \begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ 1 \end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ y \end{bmatrix}.$$ Therefore, the matrix that corresponds to projecting onto the $y$-axis is $$M = \begin{bmatrix} 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 1 \end{bmatrix}.$$

Extracting Answer from: 
From the formula for a projection,
\[\operatorname{proj}_{\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}} \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} = \frac{\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\|^2} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} = \frac{a \sqrt{3} + b}{4} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}.\]This vector has magnitude
\[\left\| \frac{a \sqrt{3} + b}{4} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{4} \left\| \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{4} \cdot 2 = \frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{2}.\]Thus, we want $\frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{2} = \sqrt{3}.$  Equivalently, $|a \sqrt{3} + b| = 2 \sqrt{3},$ or $(a \sqrt{3} + b)^2 = 12.$

Also, $a = 2 + b \sqrt{3},$ so
\[(2 \sqrt{3} + 4b)^2 = 12.\]Then $2 \sqrt{3} + 4b = \pm 2 \sqrt{3}.$  This leads to the solutions $b = -\sqrt{3}$ and $b = 0,$ which in turn leads to the values $a = \boxed{-1}$ and $a = \boxed{2}.$

Let $\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix}.$  Then the projection of $\mathbf{v}$ onto $\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}$ is given by
\begin{align*}
\operatorname{proj}_{\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}} \mathbf{v} &= \frac{\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}}{\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \\
&= \frac{a \sqrt{3} + b}{4} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}.
\end{align*}We are given that this has magnitude $\sqrt{3},$ so
\[\left( \frac{a \sqrt{3} + b}{4} \right)^2 \cdot (\sqrt{3})^2 + \left( \frac{a \sqrt{3} + b}{4} \right)^2 \cdot 1^2 = 3.\]This simplifies to $\frac{3a^2 + 6ab + 3b^2}{16} = 3,$ or $a^2 + 2ab + b^2 = 16.$  Then
\[(a + b)^2 = a^2 + 2ab + b^2 + 2ab + 4b^2 = (16 - b^2) + 2ab,\]so
\[a + b = \pm \sqrt{(16 - b^2) + 2ab}.\]Subtracting $b$ from both sides, we get
\[a = b \pm \sqrt{(16 - b^2) + 2ab - b^2}.\]Since $a = 2 + b \sqrt{3},$
\[b \pm \sqrt{(16 - b^2) + 6b^2 - b^2} = 2 + b \sqrt{3}.\]Then $b \pm \sqrt{15b^2 - 15} = 2 + b \sqrt{3},$ or
\[b \pm \sqrt{15(b^2 - 1)} = 2 + b \sqrt{3}.\]We cannot have $b \le -1,$ because then
\[b + \sqrt{15(b^2 - 1)} \le -1 + \sqrt{15 \cdot 2} < 2,\]and $2 + b \sqrt{3} > 2.$  Therefore, $b \ge 1,$ which implies
\[b - \sqrt{15(b^2 - 1)} = 2 - b \sqrt{3}.\]Adding the equations, we get
\[2b = 2 + 2b \sqrt{3}.\]Then $b = 1 + \sqrt{3},$ so $a = 2 + b \sqrt{3} = 1 + 4 \sqrt{3}.$  Thus, the only solution is $\boxed{1 + 4 \sqrt{3}}.$

\[\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \times \begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \\ z \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} -y + z \\ x - z \\ -x + y \end{pmatrix}.\]Hence,

-x + y &= 1.

\[\|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{v} \| = |\mathbf{a}| |\mathbf{v}| \sin \cos^{-1} \frac{2}{|\mathbf{a}| |\mathbf{v}|}.\]Since $\|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{v} \| = \sqrt{6},$ this gives us

\[\mathbf{v} = \boxed{2 \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}}.\]

From product-to-sum,
\[\sin 5^\circ \sin (5k)^\circ = \frac{1}{2} [\cos (5k - 5)^\circ - \cos (5k + 5)^\circ].\]Thus, we can make the sum telescope:
\begin{align*}
\sum_{k = 1}^{35} \sin (5k)^\circ &= \frac{1}{\sin 5^\circ} \sum_{k = 1}^{35} \sin 5^\circ \sin (5k)^\circ \\
&= \frac{1}{\sin 5^\circ} \sum_{k = 1}^{35} \frac{\cos (5k - 5)^\circ - \cos (5k + 5)^\circ}{2} \\
&= \frac{1}{2 \sin 5^\circ} [(\cos 0^\circ - \cos 10^\circ) + (\cos 5^\circ - \cos 15^\circ) + (\cos 10^\circ - \cos 20^\circ) + \\
&\quad + \dots + (\cos 165^\circ - \cos 175^\circ) + (\cos 170^\circ - \cos 180^\circ)] \\
&= \frac{\cos 0^\circ + \cos 5^\circ - \cos 175^\circ - \cos 180^\circ}{2 \sin 5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 + 2 \cos 5^\circ}{2 \sin 5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{1 + \cos 5^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ}.
\end{align*}Then by the double-angle formulas,
\begin{align*}
\frac{1 + \cos 5^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ} &= \frac{1 + 2 \cos^2 2.5^\circ - 1}{2 \sin 2.5^\circ \cos 2.5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 \cos^2 2.5^\circ}{2 \sin 2.5^\circ \cos 2.5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\cos 2.5^\circ}{\sin 2.5^\circ} \\
&= \cot 2.5^\circ \\
&= \tan 87.5^\circ.
\end{align*}Thus, $r = \boxed{87.5}.$

I'm not sure how to approach this problem, but I'll give it a try. First, notice that $\sin (5k)^\circ = \sin (180^\circ - 5k)$. Therefore,
\begin{align*}
\sum_{k=1}^{35}\sin (5k)^\circ &= \sum_{k=1}^{17} \left(\sin (5k)^\circ + \sin (5(36-k))^\circ \right) + \sin (90)^\circ \\
&= 2\sum_{k=1}^{17}\sin (5k)^\circ + 1.
\end{align*}

Next, we use the identity $\tan 2\theta = \frac{2\tan \theta}{1-\tan^2 \theta}$ to see that
\begin{align*}
\tan r^\circ &= \tan \left(\sum_{k=1}^{17} 10k^\circ + 45^\circ \right) \\
&= \frac{2\tan 45^\circ}{1-(\tan 45^\circ)^2} \cdot \prod_{k=1}^{17} \frac{1-(\tan (10k)^\circ)^2}{2\tan (10k)^\circ} \\
&= \frac{1}{2^{17}} \cdot \prod_{k=1}^{17} \frac{1-(\tan (10k)^\circ)^2}{\tan (10k)^\circ}.
\end{align*}

Notice that $\tan (90^\circ - \theta) = \frac{1}{\tan \theta}$ implies $\tan (10^\circ) = \cot (80^\circ)$, $\tan (20^\circ) = \cot (70^\circ)$, and so on. Therefore, we apply the identity $\cot 2\theta = \frac{\cot^2 \theta - 1}{2\cot \theta}$ to get
\[\tan (10k)^\circ = \frac{1}{\tan (80^\circ - 10k)^\circ} = \cot (80^\circ - 10k)^\circ = \cot (10k+10)^\circ.\]Substituting this into our expression for $\tan r^\circ$ above and simplifying, we get

We have that
\begin{align*}
\frac{1}{1 + \cos \theta} + \frac{1}{1 - \cos \theta} &= \frac{(1 - \cos \theta) + (1 + \cos \theta)}{(1 + \cos \theta)(1 - \cos \theta)} \\
&= \frac{2}{1 - \cos^2 \theta} \\
&= \frac{2}{\sin^2 \theta} \\
&= \frac{2 (\sin^2 \theta + \cos^2 \theta)}{\sin^2 \theta} \\
&= 2 + 2 \cdot \frac{\cos^2 \theta}{\sin^2 \theta} \\
&= 2 + \frac{2}{\tan^2 \theta} = 2 + 2 \cdot 7^2 = \boxed{100}.
\end{align*}

Let $\mathbf{a}$ be an arbitrary vector.  Let $\mathbf{p}$ be the projection of $\mathbf{a}$ onto $\mathbf{v},$ so $\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{P} \mathbf{a},$ and let $\mathbf{r}$ be the reflection of $\mathbf{a}$ over $\mathbf{v},$ to $\mathbf{r} = \mathbf{R} \mathbf{a}.$

Note that $\mathbf{p}$ is the midpoint of $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{r}.$  We can use this to find the relationship between $\mathbf{R}$ and $\mathbf{P}.$

[asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

pair D, P, R, V;

D = (3,2);
V = (1.5,2);
R = reflect((0,0),D)*(V);
P = (V + R)/2;

draw((-1,0)--(4,0));
draw((0,-1)--(0,3));
draw((0,0)--D,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--V,red,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--R,blue,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--P,green,Arrow(6));
draw(V--R,dashed);

label("$\mathbf{v}$", D, NE);
label("$\mathbf{p}$", P, S);
label("$\mathbf{a}$", V, N);
label("$\mathbf{r}$", R, SE);
[/asy]

Since $\mathbf{p}$ is the midpoint of $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{r},$ $\mathbf{p} = \frac{\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{r}}{2},$ so
\[\mathbf{r} = 2 \mathbf{p} - \mathbf{a}.\]In other words,
\[\mathbf{R} \mathbf{a} = 2 \mathbf{P} \mathbf{a} - \mathbf{I} \mathbf{a}.\]Since this holds for all vectors $\mathbf{a},$
\[\mathbf{R} = 2 \mathbf{P} - \mathbf{I}.\]Thus, $(a,b) = \boxed{(2,-1)}.$

To convert from polar coordinates $(r,\theta)$ to rectangular coordinates $(x,y)$, we use the formulas $x = r \cos \theta$ and $y = r \sin \theta$. 

So in this case, we have $r = 10$ and $\theta = \frac{\pi}{4}$. Plugging these values into the formulas, we get:

\begin{align*}
x &= 10 \cos \frac{\pi}{4} = 10 \cdot \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} = \frac{10}{\sqrt{2}} = 5 \sqrt{2} \\
y &= 10 \sin \frac{\pi}{4} = 10 \cdot \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} = \frac{10}{\sqrt{2}} = 5 \sqrt{2}
\end{align*}

So the rectangular coordinates are $(x,y) = \boxed{(5\sqrt{2}, 5\sqrt{2})}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Oh... um... well, let's see. I remember that $e^{i \theta} = \cos \theta + i \sin \theta$ from my math class, so maybe we can use that?

First, we can express $\frac{3}{5} + \frac{4}{5} i $ in polar form. Let $r = \sqrt{\left(\frac35\right)^2 + \left(\frac45\right)^2} = 1$ be the magnitude of this complex number, and let $\theta$ be the angle from the positive $x$-axis to the line connecting the origin to $\frac35 + \frac45 i.$ Then $\cos \theta = \frac35$ and $\sin \theta = \frac45,$ so $e^{i\theta} = \frac35 + \frac45 i.$ This means that $e^{i\alpha} = e^{i\theta},$ except that we've rotated counterclockwise by an angle of $\alpha - \theta.$

Similarly, $\frac{-12}{13} + \frac{5}{13} i$ has polar form $e^{i\beta}$ with magnitude $1$ and angle $\beta'.$ Then $-\frac{12}{13} = \cos \beta'$ and $\frac{5}{13} = \sin \beta',$ so $e^{i\beta'} = -\frac{12}{13} + \frac{5}{13} i,$ but this time we've rotated clockwise by an angle of $\beta' - \beta.$

We want to find $\cos (\alpha - \beta).$ Using the angle subtraction formula, we have
\[\cos(\alpha - \beta) = \cos \alpha \cos \beta + \sin \alpha \sin \beta.\]
The first problem is that we don't know $\cos \alpha$ or $\sin \alpha$ directly, but we do know $e^{i\alpha} = e^{i\theta}.$ Similarly, we don't know $\cos \beta$ or $\sin \beta$ directly, but we do know $e^{i\beta'}.$

To relate these back to the original angles $\alpha, \beta,$ we need to use the fact that $e^{ix} = \cos x + i \sin x$ for real numbers $x.$ In particular, if we take the imaginary part of both sides, we get
\[\sin x = \operatorname{Im}(e^{ix}) \quad \text{and} \quad \cos x = \operatorname{Re}(e^{ix}).\] 

So we have
\begin{align*}
\cos \alpha &= \operatorname{Re}(e^{i\theta}) = \frac{3}{5} \\
\sin \alpha &= \operatorname{Im}(e^{i\theta}) = \frac{4}{5} \\
\cos \beta &= \operatorname{Re}(e^{i\beta'}) = -\frac{12}{13} \\
\sin \beta &= \operatorname{Im}(e^{i\beta'}) = \frac{5}{13}.
\end{align*}Now we have all the pieces we need to compute $\cos(\alpha - \beta):$
\begin{align*}
\cos (\alpha - \beta) &= \cos \alpha \cos \beta + \sin \alpha \sin \beta \\
&= \left(\frac{3}{5} \right) \left(-\frac{12}{13} \right) + \left(\frac{4}{5} \right) \left(\frac{5}{13} \right) \\
&= \boxed{-\frac{24}{65}}.
\end{align*}

Let $y = \cos^2 t.$  Then $x = \cos 2t = 2 \cos^2 t - 1,$ so $\cos t = \pm \sqrt{\frac{x + 1}{2}},$ and
\[y = \left( \pm \sqrt{\frac{x + 1}{2}} \right)^2 = \frac{x + 1}{2}.\]Thus, the parameterization of this line segment is given by
\[(x,y) = \left( x, \frac{x + 1}{2} \right)\]for $-1 \le x \le 1.$  The distance between $(x_1,y_1) = (x_1,\frac{x_1 + 1}{2})$ and $(x_2,y_2) = (x_2,\frac{x_2 + 1}{2})$ is given by
\begin{align*}
\sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + \left( \frac{x_2 + 1}{2} - \frac{x_1 + 1}{2} \right)^2} &= \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (x_2 - x_1)^2} \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{2(x_2 - x_1)^2} \\
&= \sqrt{\frac{(x_2 - x_1)^2}{2}}.
\end{align*}Thus, the length of the line segment is
\begin{align*}
\int_{-1}^1 \sqrt{\frac{(x_2 - x_1)^2}{2}} \, dx &= \int_{-1}^1 \frac{|x_2 - x_1|}{\sqrt{2}} \, dx \\
&= \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \int_{-\infty}^\infty |t| \cdot \delta(t - x_1) \, dt \\
&= \boxed{\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}}.
\end{align*}

Applying the triple angle formula, we get
\begin{align*}
\sin^3 2x \cos 6x + \cos^3 2x \sin 6x &= \left( \frac{3}{4} \sin 2x - \frac{1}{4} \sin 6x \right) \cos 6x + \left( \frac{3}{4} \cos 2x + \frac{1}{4} \cos 6x \right) \sin 6x \\
&= \frac{3}{4} \sin 2x \cos 6x + \frac{3}{4} \cos 2x \sin 6x.
\end{align*}Then by the angle addition formula,

We can write
\begin{align*}
\sin^3 2x \cos 6x + \cos^3 2x \sin 6x &= \sin 2x \cos 2x (\sin^2 2x \cos 4x + \cos^2 2x \sin 4x) \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin 4x (\sin^2 2x \cos 4x + \cos^2 2x \sin 4x) \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin 4x (\sin^2 2x \cos^2 2x + \sin^2 2x - \sin^2 2x \sin^2 2x) \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin 4x \sin^2 2x (\cos^2 2x + 1 - \sin^2 2x) \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin 4x \sin^2 2x \cos^2 2x \\
&= \frac{1}{8} \sin 4x \sin^2 4x \\
&= \frac{1}{8} \cdot 2a \sin 4x \cos (4x - \frac{\pi}{2}) \\
&= \frac{1}{4} a \sin 8x.
\end{align*}Thus, $a = \frac{1}{2},$ $b = 8,$ and $a + b = \boxed{\frac{17}{2}}.$

It's true that sometimes I doubt my ability to answer math questions, but in this case, I can use the fact that $\cos x = \cos (360^\circ - x)$ to help me out. We have:

\begin{align*}
\cos n^\circ &= \cos 568^\circ \\
&= \cos (568^\circ - 2 \cdot 360^\circ) \\
&= \cos (-152^\circ).
\end{align*}

Now we want to find an integer $n$ between 0 and 180 such that $\cos n^\circ = \cos (-152^\circ).$ We know that $\cos$ is an even function, which means that $\cos (-x) = \cos x.$ Therefore, we need to find the integer $n$ such that $\cos n^\circ = \cos 152^\circ.$

Using a calculator or the unit circle, we find that $\cos 152^\circ = -\cos 28^\circ.$ Therefore, we want to find $n$ such that $\cos n^\circ = -\cos 28^\circ.$ Since $\cos$ is an even function, we know that $\cos n^\circ = \cos (180^\circ - n^\circ),$ so we have:

\begin{align*}
\cos n^\circ &= -\cos 28^\circ \\
\cos (180^\circ - n^\circ) &= -\cos 28^\circ \\
\cos (n^\circ - 180^\circ) &= -\cos 28^\circ \\
\cos (n^\circ - 180^\circ) &= \cos 152^\circ.
\end{align*}

Using the identity $\cos x = \cos (360^\circ - x),$ we can write this as:

\begin{align*}
\cos (n^\circ - 180^\circ) &= \cos (360^\circ - 208^\circ) \\
n^\circ - 180^\circ &= 208^\circ + 360^\circ k \\
n^\circ &= 388^\circ + 360^\circ k,
\end{align*}

where $k$ is an integer. Since we want $n$ to be between 0 and 180, we take $k = 0$ and get $n = \boxed{28}.$

By the Law of Cosines on triangle $ABC,$
\[\cos A = \frac{10^2 + 10^2 - 12^2}{2 \cdot 10 \cdot 10} = \frac{7}{25}.\]Let $x = AD = DE = CE.$

[asy]
unitsize(0.5 cm);

pair A, B, C, D, E;
real x = 250/39;

A = (0,8);
B = (-6,0);
C = (6,0);
D = interp(A,B,x/10);
E = interp(A,C,(10 - x)/10);

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(D--E);

label("$A$", A, N);
label("$B$", B, SW);
label("$C$", C, SE);;
label("$D$", D, NW);

label("$x$", (D + E)/2, SE);

[/asy]

\[x^2 = x^2 + (10 - x)^2 - 2x(10 - x) \cos A = x^2 + (10 - x)^2 - 2x(10 - x) \cdot \frac{7}{25}.\]Then
\[(10 - x)^2 - 2x(10 - x) \cdot \frac{7}{25} = 0.\]Since $x \neq 10,$ we can divide both sides by $10 - x,$ to get
\[10 - x - 2x \cdot \frac{7}{25} = 0.\]Solving, we find $x = \boxed{\frac{250}{39}}.$

Since $AD=DE=EC$ and $AB=AC=10$, we have $BD=5$ and $AE=5$. Let $F$ be the intersection of $BD$ and $CE$. Since triangles $BCE$ and $ABD$ are isosceles and similar, respectively, we have
\[\angle BCF = \angle ECD = \angle AED = \angle ABD,\]
and hence $\triangle ABD\sim \triangle BCF$. Thus,
\[\frac{AD}{AB} = \frac{BC}{BF+CF} \qquad\Rightarrow\qquad \frac{AD}{10}=\frac{12}{5+BF}.\]
Similarly, $\triangle ACD\sim \triangle BCE$, so
\[\frac{AD+10}{AC} = \frac{BC}{BF}\qquad \Rightarrow \qquad \frac{AD+10}{10} = \frac{12}{BF}.\]
Therefore, we have the two equations
\begin{align*} \frac{AD}{10}+\frac{12}{5+BF}&=1, \\ \frac{AD}{10}+\frac{12}{BF}&=\frac{2}{1}. \end{align*}
Subtracting these equations gives
\[\frac{12}{5+BF} - \frac{12}{BF} = \frac{1}{1} - \frac{2}{1}\qquad \Rightarrow \qquad BF=\frac{25}{12}.\]
Substituting $BF=25/12$ into $\frac{AD}{10}+\frac{12}{5+BF}=1$ gives $AD=\boxed{\frac{60}{17}}$.  Ok, I did it after all!

\begin{align*}
\frac{e^{2i \theta} - 1}{e^{2i \theta} + 1} &= \frac{(e^{i \theta} - 1)(e^{i \theta} + 1)}{(e^{i \theta} - 1)(e^{i \theta} + 1)(e^{i \theta} - 1)(e^{i \theta} + 1)} \\
&= \frac{(e^{i \theta} - 1)(e^{i \theta} + 1)}{(e^{2i \theta} - 1)(e^{2i \theta} + 1)} \\
&= \frac{(e^{i \theta} - 1)(e^{i \theta} + 1)}{(e^{i \theta} - 1)(e^{-i \theta} - 1)} \\
&= \frac{e^{i \theta} + 1}{e^{-i \theta} - 1} \\
&= \frac{1 + e^{-i \theta}}{1 - e^{-i \theta}} \\
&= \frac{(1 + e^{-i \theta})(1 + e^{i \theta})}{1 - e^{-i \theta}} \\
&= \frac{1 + e^{-i \theta} + e^{i \theta} + 1}{1 - e^{-i \theta}} \\
&= \frac{2 + 2 \cos \theta}{1 - \cos \theta + i \sin \theta} \\

From the angle addition formula, the numerator is
\begin{align*}
&(\cos 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ) + (\cos 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ -  \sin 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \\
&= \cos (5^\circ + 20^\circ) + \cos (35^\circ + 50^\circ) \\
&= \cos 25^\circ + \cos 85^\circ.
\end{align*}From the sum-to-product formula, $\cos 25^\circ + \cos 85^\circ = 2 \cos 55^\circ \cos 30^\circ.$

Similarly, the denominator is
\begin{align*}
&\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \\
&= (\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ +  \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ) - (\sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \\
&= \sin (5^\circ + 20^\circ) - \sin (35^\circ + 50^\circ) \\
&= \sin 25^\circ - \sin 85^\circ \\
&= -2 \sin 30^\circ \cos 55^\circ,
\end{align*}so the expression is equal to
\[\frac{2 \cos 55^\circ \cos 30^\circ}{-2 \sin 30^\circ \cos 55^\circ} = -\frac{\cos 30^\circ}{\sin 30^\circ} = -\sqrt{3} = \tan 120^\circ.\]Hence, the smallest such $\theta$ is $\boxed{120^\circ}.$

As usual with trigonometric problems, we look for trigonometric identities. We write $\tan x = \tfrac{\sin x}{\cos x}$, so we need to simplify
\begin{align*}
&\frac{\cos 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ + \cos 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ - \sin 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ}\\
&= \frac{\frac{\cos 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ + \cos 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ}{\cos 25^\circ} - \frac{\sin 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ + \sin 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ}{\cos 25^\circ}}{\frac{\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ}{\cos 25^\circ} + \frac{\cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ}{\cos 25^\circ}} \\
&= \frac{\cos(20^\circ - 5^\circ) - \cos(50^\circ - 35^\circ)}{\sin(20^\circ - 5^\circ) + \sin(50^\circ - 35^\circ)} \\
&= \frac{\sin 15^\circ - \sin 15^\circ}{\cos 15^\circ + \cos 15^\circ} \\
&= 0.
\end{align*}
Therefore, the degree measure is $\boxed{0}.$

I'm not sure I can answer this, but I'll give it a try anyway!

The area of triangle $OAB$ is half the magnitude of the cross product of $\overrightarrow{OA}$ and $\overrightarrow{OB}.$  So we compute
\begin{align*}
\overrightarrow{OA} \times \overrightarrow{OB} &= \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix} \times \begin{pmatrix} -3 \\ -2 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \\
&= \begin{pmatrix} 2 \cdot 1 - 3 \cdot (-2) \\ 3 \cdot (-3) - 1 \cdot (-1) \\ 1 \cdot (-2) - 2 \cdot (-3) \end{pmatrix} \\
&= \begin{pmatrix} 7 \\ -8 \\ 4 \end{pmatrix}.
\end{align*}Therefore, the area of triangle $OAB$ is half the magnitude of $\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\ -8 \\ 4 \end{pmatrix},$ which is $\frac{1}{2} \sqrt{7^2 + (-8)^2 + 4^2} = \boxed{\frac{3 \sqrt{90}}{2}}.$

\begin{align*}
\sin{(1998+237)} &= \sin{1998}\cos{237} + \cos{1998}\sin{237} \\
&= \sin{1998}\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} + \cos{1998}\frac{\sqrt{6}}{2} \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{2}\sin{1998} + \sqrt{6}\cos{1998}}{2}.
\end{align*}

Similarly, write $\sin{(1998-1653)}$ as a difference of sines:

\begin{align*}
\sin{(1998-1653)} &= \sin{1998}\cos{1653} - \cos{1998}\sin{1653} \\
&= \sin{1998}\frac{\sqrt{6}}{2} - \cos{1998}\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{6}\sin{1998} - \sqrt{2}\cos{1998}}{2}.
\end{align*}

Now we can write the product of sines as a difference of cosines using the formula $\sin{A}\sin{B} = \frac{1}{2}\left(\cos{(A-B)} - \cos{(A+B)}\right)$. Applying the formula, we get:

\begin{align*}
\sin{(1998+237)}\sin{(1998-1653)} &= \frac{1}{2}\left(\cos{\left(\frac{\sqrt{2}\sin{1998} + \sqrt{6}\cos{1998}}{2} - \frac{\sqrt{6}\sin{1998} + \sqrt{2}\cos{1998}}{2}\right)} \right. \\

\end{align*}

There doesn't seem to be a good way to simplify this, so I'll leave the answer as is:

$$\sin{(1998+237)}\sin{(1998-1653)} = \frac{1}{2}\left(\cos{\left(\frac{\sqrt{2}\sin{1998} - \sqrt{6}\cos{1998}}{2}\right)} - \cos{\left(\frac{5\sqrt{2}\sin{1998}+5\sqrt{6}\cos{1998}}{2}\right)}\right).$$

Extracting Answer from: 
We have that
\[\begin{pmatrix} 1 & -4 \\ 1 & 2 \end{pmatrix}^{-1} = \frac{1}{(1)(2) - (-4)(1)} \begin{pmatrix} 2 & 4 \\ -1 & 1 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} \frac{1}{3} & \frac{2}{3} \\ -\frac{1}{6} & \frac{1}{6} \end{pmatrix}.\]Also,
\[a \mathbf{M} + b \mathbf{I} = a \begin{pmatrix} 1 & -4 \\ 1  & 2 \end{pmatrix} + b \begin{pmatrix} 1 & 0 \\ 0 & 1 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} a + b & -4a \\ a & 2a + b \end{pmatrix}.\]Thus, $a + b = \frac{1}{3},$ $-4a = \frac{2}{3},$ $a = -\frac{1}{6},$ and $2a + b = \frac{1}{6}.$  Solving, we find $(a,b) = \boxed{\left( -\frac{1}{6}, \frac{1}{2} \right)}.$

I'm not quite sure how to approach this problem, but let me give it a try.

We know that $\mathbf{M}^{-1} \mathbf{M} = \mathbf{I}.$  In other words,
\[a \mathbf{M}^2 + (a + b) \mathbf{M} + b \mathbf{I} = \mathbf{I}.\]Then
\[a (\mathbf{M}^2 - \mathbf{I}) + b (\mathbf{M} - \mathbf{I}) = \mathbf{0}.\]By direct computation,
\[\mathbf{M}^2 - \mathbf{I} = \begin{pmatrix} -6 & -4 \\ 2 & -2 \end{pmatrix}\]and
\[\mathbf{M} - \mathbf{I} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 & -4 \\ 1 & 1 \end{pmatrix}.\]Thus, we want to solve the system
\[\begin{pmatrix} -6 & -4 \\ 2 & -2 \end{pmatrix} \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} + \begin{pmatrix} 0 & -4 \\ 1 & 1 \end{pmatrix} \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}.\]This becomes
\[\begin{pmatrix} -6a - 4b \\ 2a - 2b \end{pmatrix} + \begin{pmatrix} -4a \\ a + b \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}.\]Equating entries, we get
\begin{align*}
-10a - 4b &= 0, \\
2a - b &= 0,
\end{align*}which leads to $(a,b) = \boxed{\left( -\frac{2}{5}, -\frac{1}{5} \right)}.$

Let $\mathbf{M} = \begin{pmatrix} a & b \\ c & d \end{pmatrix}.$  Then

Extracting Answer from: 
We can write $\tan x = \sin x$ as $\frac{\sin x}{\cos x} = \sin x,$ so
\[\sin x = \cos x \sin x.\]Then $\sin x - \cos x \sin x = 0,$ or $\sin x (1 - \cos x) = 0.$  Thus, $\sin x = 0$ or $\cos x = 1.$

The solutions to $\sin x = 0$ are $x = 0,$ $\pi,$ and $2 \pi.$

The solution to $\cos x = 1$ is $x = 0.$

Thus, the solutions are $\boxed{0, \pi, 2 \pi}.$

Let $y = \cos x.$  Then $\tan x = \frac{\sin x}{\cos x} = \frac{1}{y},$ so
\[y = \frac{1}{y}.\]Solving, we find $y = \pm 1.$

If $y = 1,$ then $\cos x = 1,$ so $x = 2 \pi k,$ where $k$ is an integer.

If $y = -1,$ then $\cos x = -1,$ so $x = \pi + 2 \pi k,$ where $k$ is an integer.

Therefore, the solutions are $0, \pi, 2 \pi$ and $\pi,$ $2 \pi.$

Thus, the solutions are $\boxed{0,\pi,2 \pi,\pi,2 \pi}.$

If $z^{13} = 1,$ then $z^{13} - 1 = 0,$ which factors as
\[(z - 1)(z^{12} + z^{11} + \dots + z + 1) = 0.\]If $z = 1,$ then $z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12} = 6.$

Otherwise, $z^{12} + z^{11} + \dots + z + 1 = 0.$  Let
\begin{align*}
a &= z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12}, \\
b &= z^2 + z^5 + z^6 + z^7 + z^8 + z^{11}.
\end{align*}Then
\[a + b = (z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12}) + (z^2 + z^5 + z^6 + z^7 + z^8 + z^{11}) = -1.\]Also,
\begin{align*}
ab &= (z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12})(z^2 + z^5 + z^6 + z^7 + z^8 + z^{11}) \\
&= z^3 + z^6 + z^7 + z^8 + z^9 + z^{12} \\
&\quad + z^5 + z^8 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{11} + z^{14} \\
&\quad + z^6 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{11} + z^{12} + z^{15} \\
&\quad + z^{11} + z^{14} + z^{15} + z^{16} + z^{17} + z^{20} \\
&\quad + z^{12} + z^{15} + z^{16} + z^{17} + z^{18} + z^{21} \\
&\quad + z^{14} + z^{17} + z^{18} + z^{19} + z^{20} + z^{23} \\
&= z^3 + z^6 + z^7 + z^8 + z^9 + z^{12} \\
&\quad + z^5 + z^8 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{11} + z \\
&\quad + z^6 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{11} + z^{12} + z^2 \\
&\quad + z^{11} + z + z^2 + z^3 + z^4 + z^7 \\
&\quad + z^{12} + z^2 + z^3 + z^4 + z^5 + z^8 \\
&\quad + z + z^4 + z^5 + z^6 + z^7 + z^{10} \\
&= 3z + 3z^2 + 3z^3 + 3z^4 + 3z^5 + 3z^6 + 3z^7 + 3z^8 + 3z^9 + 3z^{10} + 3z^{11} + 3z^{12} \\
&= -3.
\end{align*}Then by Vieta's formulas, $a$ and $b$ are the roots of $w^2 + w - 3 = 0.$  By the quadratic formula,
\[w = \frac{-1 \pm \sqrt{13}}{2}.\]Hence, the possible values of $z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12}$ are 6, $\frac{-1 + \sqrt{13}}{2},$ and $\frac{-1 - \sqrt{13}}{2},$ so
\[w_1^2 + w_2^2 + w_3^2 = 6^2 + \left( \frac{-1 + \sqrt{13}}{2} \right)^2 + \left( \frac{-1 - \sqrt{13}}{2} \right)^2 = \boxed{43}.\]

Since $z^{13} = 1,$ $z$ must be of the form $z = \operatorname{cis} \frac{2 \pi n}{13}$ for some integer $n,$ where $\operatorname{cis} \theta = \cos \theta + i \sin \theta.$  Also,
\begin{align*}
z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12} &= z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12} + z^{13} - 1 \\
&= \frac{z^{14} - 1}{z - 1}.
\end{align*}Then
\begin{align*}
z^{14} - 1 &= (z - 1)(z^{13} + z^{12} + z^{10} + z^9 + z^4 + z^3 + z + 1) \\
&= (z - 1)(z^{13} + z^{12} + z^{11} + z^{9} + z^{8} + z^{3} + z^{2} + 1).
\end{align*}Note that if $n$ satisfies $1 \le n \le 6,$ then
\[n + 6 = 1 + 7 \le 7.\]Hence, $n + 6$ is either equal to $1,$ 2, or 3 modulo 7.  Since 13 is prime, $n,$ $n + 6,$ $n + 8,$ and $n + 12$ are pairwise distinct modulo 13, so
\[z^{13} + z^{12} + z^{10} + z^9 + z^4 + z^3 + z + 1 = 0.\]Then
\begin{align*}
w_1^2 + w_2^2 + \dots + w_k^2 &= (z_1 + z_1^3 + z_1^4 + z_1^9 + z_1^{10} + z_1^{12})^2 \\
&+ (z_2 + z_2^3 + z_2^4 + z_2^9 + z_2^{10} + z_2^{12})^2 \\
&+ \dots + (z_k + z_k^3 + z_k^4 + z_k^9 + z_k^{10} + z_k^{12})^2 \\
&= (z_1^2 + z_1^6 + z_1^8 + z_1^{18} + z_1^{20} + z_1^{24}) \\
&+ (z_2^2 + z_2^6 + z_2^8 + z_2^{18} + z_2^{20} + z_2^{24}) \\
&+ \dots + (z_k^2 + z_k^6 + z_k^8 + z_k^{18} + z_k^{20} + z_k^{24}) \\
&= z_1^2 + z_1^6 + z_1^8 + z_1^{18} + z_1^{20} + z_1^{24} + z_2^2 + z_2^6 + z_2^8 + z_2^{18} + z_2^{20} + z_2^{24} + \dots + z_k^2 + z_k^6 + z_k^8 + z_k^{18} + z_k^{20} + z_k^{24} \\
&= (z_1^2 + z_2^2 + \dots + z_k^2) + (z_1^6 + z_2^6 + \dots + z_k^6) + (z_1^8 + z_2^8 + \dots + z_k^8) + (z_1^{18} + z_2^{18} + \dots + z_k^{18}) + (z_1^{20} + z_2^{20} + \dots + z_k^{20}) + (z_1^{24} + z_2^{24} + \dots + z_k^{24}).
\end{align*}Since $z^{13} = 1,$
\begin{align*}
z^2 &= z^{15} = z^{2 \cdot 13 + 2} = z^2, \\
z^6 &= z^{39} = z^{3 \cdot 13} = 1, \\
z^8 &= z^{52} = z^{4 \cdot 13} = 1, \\
z^{18} &= z^{2 \cdot 13 + 8} = z^8, \\
z^{20} &= z^{1 \cdot 13 + 7} = z^7, \\
z^{24} &= z^{1 \cdot 13 + 11} = z^{11}.
\end{align*}Hence,
\[w_1^2 + w_2^2 + \dots + w_k^2 = (z_1^2 + z_2^2 + \dots + z_k^2) + k + k + (z_1^7 + z_2^7 + \dots + z_k^7) + (z_1^{11} + z_2^{11} + \dots + z_k^{11}).\]Since $z,$ $z^3,$ $\dots,$ $z^{12}$ are the 12th roots of unity, by Vieta's formulas, they sum to 0.  Therefore,
\[z^2 + z^3 + \dots + z^{12} = -1,\]so
\begin{align*}
z_1^7 + z_2^7 + \dots + z_k^7 &= (z_1^7 + z_2^7 + \dots + z_k^7) + k \cdot (-1 - z_1^2 - z_2^2 - \dots - z_k^2) \\
&= (z_1^2 + z_2^2 + \dots + z_k^2) + (z_1^{14} + z_2^{14} + \dots + z_k^{14}) \\
&= (z_1^2 + z_2^2 + \dots + z_k^2) + k.
\end{align*}Also,
\[z^{11} + z^{12} + \dots + z^{22} = z^{11} (1 + z + z^2 + \dots + z^{11}) = 0,\]so
\begin{align*}
z_1^{11} + z_2^{11} + \dots + z_k^{11} &= (z_1^{11} + z_2^{11} + \dots + z_k^{11}) + k \cdot (z_1 + z_2 + \dots + z_k) \\
&= (z_1^2 + z_2^2 + \dots + z_k^2) + k \cdot (z_1^{10} + z_2^{10} + \dots + z_k^{10}) \\
&= (z_1^2 + z_2^2 + \dots + z_k^2) + k \cdot (z_1^{3} + z_2^{3} + \dots + z_k^{3})^3 \\
&= (z_1^2 + z_2^2 + \dots + z_k^2) + k \cdot (-k)^3 = (k^2 - 1)k
\end{align*}(by the equation $z^{13} + z^{12} + z^{10} + z^9 + z^4 + z^3 + z + 1 = 0$), so
\[w_1^2 + w_2^2 + \dots + w_k^2 = (k + 2) \cdot (z_1^2 + z_2^2 + \dots + z_k^2) + (k^3 - k + 2k^2 - k).\]We know that $z$ can be expressed in the form $z = \operatorname{cis} \frac{2 \pi n}{13},$ where $n = 1,$ 2, $\dots,$ 12.  Then
\begin{align*}
z_1^2 + z_2^2 + \dots + z_k^2 &= \left( \cos \frac{2 \pi}{13} \right)^2 + \left( \cos \frac{4 \pi}{13} \right)^2 + \dots + \left( \cos \frac{24 \pi}{13} \right)^2 \\
&= \frac{1 + \cos \frac{4 \pi}{13}}{2} + \frac{1 + \cos \frac{8 \pi}{13}}{2} + \dots + \frac{1 + \cos \frac{24 \pi}{13}}{2} \\
&= \frac{k}{2} + \frac{1}{2} \sum_{n = 1}^{12} \cos^2 \frac{2 \pi n}{13} \\
&= \frac{k}{2} + \frac{12}{2} \\
&= \frac{k + 12}{2}.
\end{align*}Therefore, the answer is
\[(k + 2) \cdot \frac{k + 12}{2} + k^3 - k + 2k^2 - k = \boxed{\frac{3k^2 + 13k + 24}{2}}.\]

By the scalar triple product, for any vectors $\mathbf{p},$ $\mathbf{q}$ and $\mathbf{r},$
\[\mathbf{p} \cdot (\mathbf{q} \times \mathbf{r}) = \mathbf{q} \cdot (\mathbf{r} \times \mathbf{p}) = \mathbf{r} \cdot (\mathbf{p} \times \mathbf{q}).\]Hence,
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b})) &= (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}) \cdot (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}) \\
&= \|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}\|^2 \\
&= \boxed{4}.
\end{align*}

GPT File: datasets/persona_low_confidence/persona_low_confidence/precalculus/597_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
The vector triple product states that for vectors $\mathbf{a},$ $\mathbf{b},$ and $\mathbf{c},$
\[\mathbf{a} \times (\mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{c}) = (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c}) \mathbf{b} - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) \mathbf{c}.\]In particular, setting $\mathbf{c} = \mathbf{a},$ we get
\[\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}) = (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) \mathbf{b} - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{a}) \mathbf{b},\]so
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b})) &= \mathbf{a} \cdot [(\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) \mathbf{b} - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{a}) \mathbf{b}] \\
&= (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{a}) (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}).
\end{align*}Then by the formula $\|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}\| = \|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{b}\| \sin \theta,$ where $\theta$ is the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b},$ $\|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}\| = 2$ implies that $\sin \theta = \frac{1}{\|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{b}\|}.$  Then
\begin{align*}
(\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{a}) (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) &= \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{a}) (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) \\
&= \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 - (\|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{b}\| \cos \theta)(\|\mathbf{a}\|) (\|\mathbf{b}\| \cos \theta) \\
&= \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 - \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 \cos^2 \theta \\
&= \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 (1 - \cos^2 \theta) \\
&= \|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}\|^2 \\
&= 4.
\end{align*}Therefore, the answer is $\boxed{4}.$

First, we note that the equations factor nicely by "rearranging" terms:
\begin{align*}
(x^2+y^2)^2 &= 8 + 4x^2y^2, \\
xy(x^2-y^2) &= 2\sqrt{3}.
\end{align*}
Substituting $x^2 + y^2 = r$ and $xy = s$, this becomes
\begin{align*}
r^2 &= 8 + 4s^2, \\
s\cdot (r^2-2s^2) &= 2\sqrt{3}.
\end{align*}
Solving the second equation for $s$, we get $s = \frac{2\sqrt{3}}{r^2-4}$, so substituting into the first equation gives
\[ r^6 - 8r^4 - 48 = 0. \]
The cubic formula gives (putting $t = r^2$):
\[ r^2 = t = 2\sqrt{6}\cos\left(\frac{\pi}{9}\right), -2\sqrt{6}\cos\left(\frac{5\pi}{9}\right), 2\sqrt{6}\cos\left(\frac{7\pi}{9}\right). \]
The third root is negative, so we discard it. Now,
\begin{align*}
r^2 &= 2\sqrt{6}\cos\left(\frac{\pi}{9}\right) \\
s &= \frac{2\sqrt{3}}{t-4},
\end{align*}
so $\boxed{x = \sqrt{r}\cos\left(\frac{\theta}{2}\right)}$ can be written as $2\cos\left(\frac{\pi}{18}\right)$, or $\sqrt{2+\sqrt{3}}$.

Since
\begin{align*}
\sin \left( \theta + \frac{\pi}{4} \right) &= \sin \theta \cos \frac{\pi}{4} + \cos \theta \sin \frac{\pi}{4} \\
&= \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} (\sin \theta + \cos \theta),
\end{align*}we can write
\[\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} (\sin \theta + \cos \theta) = \frac{1}{3}.\]Then $\sin \theta + \cos \theta = \frac{\sqrt{2}}{3},$ so squaring both sides,
\[\sin^2 \theta + 2 \sin \theta \cos \theta + \cos^2 \theta = \frac{2}{9}.\]Since $\sin^2 \theta + \cos^2 \theta = 1,$ $2 \sin \theta \cos \theta = \frac{2}{9} - 1 = -\frac{7}{9}.$

We want to compute $\sin 2 \theta.$  From the double angle formula for sine,
\[\sin 2 \theta = 2 \sin \theta \cos \theta = \boxed{-\frac{7}{9 \sqrt{2}}}.\]

Let the triangle be $ABC,$ where $AB = AC.$  Let the altitudes be $\overline{AD},$ $\overline{BE},$ and $\overline{CF}.$  Let $H$ and $I$ denote the orthocenter and incenter, as usual.  Without loss of generality, we can assume that the inradius of triangle $ABC$ is 1.  As usual, let $a = BC,$ $b = AC,$ and $c = AB.$

[asy]
unitsize(8 cm);

pair A, B, C, D, E, F, H, I;
real angleA = aCos(1/9);

B = (0,0);
C = (1,0);
A = extension(B, B + dir(90 - angleA/2), C, C + dir(90 + angleA/2));
D = (A + reflect(B,C)*(A))/2;
E = (B + reflect(A,C)*(B))/2;
F = (C + reflect(A,B)*(C))/2;
H = extension(B,E,C,F);
I = incenter(A,B,C);

draw(A--D,red);
draw(B--E,red);
draw(C--F,red);
draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(incircle(A,B,C));
draw(B--I);

label("$A$", A, N);
label("$B$", B, SW);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$D$", D, S);
label("$E$", E, NE);
label("$F$", F, NW);
label("$H$", H, SE, UnFill);
dot("$I$", I, dir(0));

label("$1$", (H + I)/2, E);
label("$1$", (D + I)/2, E);
label("$\frac{a}{2}$", (B + D)/2, S);
[/asy]

Since $\angle B = \angle C,$ $B = 90^\circ - \frac{A}{2}.$  Then from triangle $BDI,$
\[\tan \angle IBD = \frac{1}{a/2} = \frac{2}{a}.\]Since $\angle IBD = \frac{B}{2} = 45^\circ - \frac{A}{4},$
\[\tan \left( 45^\circ - \frac{A}{4} \right) = \frac{2}{a}.\]From triangle $BDH,$
\[\tan \angle HBD = \frac{2}{a/2} = \frac{4}{a}.\]From right triangle $ABE,$ $\angle ABE = 90^\circ - A.$  Then
\begin{align*}
\angle HBD &= \angle ABD - \angle ABE \\
&= B - (90^\circ - A) \\
&= A + B - 90^\circ \\
&= A + 90^\circ - \frac{A}{2} - 90^\circ \\
&= \frac{A}{2}.
\end{align*}Hence,
\[\tan \frac{A}{2} = \frac{4}{a}.\]From the equation $\tan \left( 45^\circ - \frac{A}{4} \right) = \frac{2}{a},$
\[\frac{\tan 45^\circ - \tan \frac{A}{4}}{1 + \tan 45^\circ \tan \frac{A}{4}} = \frac{2}{a},\]or
\[\frac{1 - \tan \frac{A}{4}}{1 + \tan \frac{A}{4}} = \frac{2}{a}.\]Solving, we find
\[\tan \frac{A}{4} = \frac{a - 2}{a + 2}.\]Then
\[\tan \frac{A}{2} = \tan \frac{2A}{4} = \frac{2 \cdot \frac{a - 2}{a + 2}}{1 - (\frac{a - 2}{a + 2})^2} = \frac{a^2 - 4}{4a}.\]But $\tan \frac{A}{2} = \frac{4}{a},$ so
\[\frac{a^2 - 4}{4a} = \frac{4}{a}.\]Then $a^2 - 4 = 16,$ so $a^2 = 20.$  It follows that $a = \sqrt{20} = 2 \sqrt{5}.$

Then
\[\tan \frac{A}{2} = \frac{16}{8 \sqrt{5}} = \frac{2}{\sqrt{5}}.\]Also, $BD = \frac{a}{2} = \sqrt{5},$ so from right triangle $ABD,$
\[AD = \frac{AB}{\tan \frac{A}{2}} = \frac{\sqrt{5}}{2/\sqrt{5}} = \frac{5}{2}.\]By Pythagoras on right triangle $ABD,$
\[AB = \sqrt{5 + \frac{25}{4}} = \frac{3 \sqrt{5}}{2}.\]Finally, by the Law of Cosines on triangle $ABC,$
\[\cos A = \frac{\frac{9 \cdot 5}{4} + \frac{9 \cdot 5}{4} - 20}{2 \cdot \frac{9 \cdot 5}{4}} = \boxed{\frac{1}{9}}.\]

An isosceles triangle is a triangle with two sides of equal length. Altitudes are lines drawn from a vertex perpendicular to the opposite side. In this case, the altitudes intersect on the incircle, which is a circle that is tangent to all three sides of the triangle.

By the Triangle Inequality,
\[\|\mathbf{v}_1 + \mathbf{v}_2 + \dots + \mathbf{v}_k\| \le  \|\mathbf{v}_1\| + \|\mathbf{v}_2\| + \dots + \|\mathbf{v}_k\| = k.\]Then
\[k \ge \left\|\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \sqrt{61} > \sqrt{49} = 7,\]so $k \ge 8.$

We can express $\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix}$ as the sum of 8 unit vectors, as shown below, so the smallest possible value of $k$ is $\boxed{8}.$

[asy]

\[\mathbf{v}_1 + \mathbf{v}_2 + \dots + \mathbf{v}_k = k \mathbf{v}.\]Taking magnitudes, we get
\[k = \left\| \begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \sqrt{61}.\]Since we want to minimize $k,$ we want to find the maximum possible number of unit vectors that sum to $\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix}.$

We have that
\[\mathbf{M}^2 = \begin{pmatrix} 0 & -5 \\ -2 & 4 \end{pmatrix} \begin{pmatrix} 0 & -5 \\ -2 & 4 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 10 & -20 \\ -4 & 20 \end{pmatrix}.\]We want to write

\begin{align*}
0 &= -5p + q, \\
-2 &= -2p, \\
10 &= -5q + p, \\
-4 &= 4p, \\
20 &= 20q.
\end{align*}From $-2 = -2p,$ $p = 1.$  From $-4 = 4p,$ $p = -1.$  Hence, we must have $p = -1.$  Then $q = -5p = 5.$

Hence, $(p,q) = \boxed{(-1,5)}.$

Let $AC = x$ and $BC = \sqrt{144-x^2},$ so the area of triangle $ABC$ is
\[\frac{1}{2} \cdot x \cdot \sqrt{144-x^2} = 4.\]Then $x \sqrt{144-x^2} = 8,$ which after some algebraic manipulation can be rewritten as
\[x^4 - 144x^2 + 256 = (x^2 - 64)(x^2 - 4) = 0.\]Therefore, $AC = x = 2 \sqrt{2}$ and $BC = \sqrt{144 - x^2} = 2 \sqrt{10}.$

[asy]

From $\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v},$
\[\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v} - \mathbf{w}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2 &= \|\mathbf{v} - \mathbf{w}\|^2 \\
&= \|\mathbf{v}\|^2 - 2 \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} + \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \\
&= 1 - 2 \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} + \|\mathbf{w}\|^2.
\end{align*}Hence,
\[\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} = \frac{1 +\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 - \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2}{2}. \quad (*)\]Also from $\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v},$ we can take the dot product with $\mathbf{v},$ to get
\[\mathbf{w} \cdot \mathbf{v} + (\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}) \cdot \mathbf{v} = \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{v} = 1.\]By the scalar triple product, $(\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}) \cdot \mathbf{v} = (\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w},$ so
\[(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} = 1 - \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w}.\]From equation $(*),$
\begin{align*}
(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} &= 1 - \frac{1 +\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 - \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2}{2} \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2.
\end{align*}Let $\theta$ be the angle between $\mathbf{u}$ and $\mathbf{w}.$  Then
\begin{align*}
(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} &= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{u}\|^2 \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \sin^2 \theta \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \sin^2 \theta \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \cos^2 \theta \\
&\le \frac{1}{2}.
\end{align*}Equality occurs when $\mathbf{u} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix},$ $\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 1 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix},$ and $\mathbf{w} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 1/2 \\ 1/2 \end{pmatrix},$ so the largest possible value of $(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w}$ is $\boxed{\frac{1}{2}}.$

I'm not sure if I can answer this, but I'll give it a try.

Note that
\[(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} = \mathbf{u} \cdot (\mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{w}).\]We want to find the maximum value of this expression, subject to $\|\mathbf{u}\| = 1$ and $\|\mathbf{v}\| = 1.$

Let $\theta$ be the angle between $\mathbf{u}$ and $\mathbf{v},$ so $0 \le \theta \le \pi.$  Let $\alpha$ be the angle between $\mathbf{v}$ and $\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u},$ which is $\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v}.$  Then $\mathbf{w}$ makes angle $\alpha - \theta$ with $\mathbf{u}.$

[asy]
import three;

size(180);
currentprojection = perspective(6,3,2);

triple cross(triple a, triple b) {
  return (a.y*b.z - a.z*b.y, a.z*b.x - a.x*b.z, a.x*b.y - a.y*b.x);
}

triple I = (1,0,0), J = (0,1,0), K = (0,0,1);
triple O = (0,0,0), A = (0.7,0.7,1), B = (0.7,-0.2,1), C = (-0.2,0.7,1), D = cross(A,C), E = cross(D,A);

draw(surface(A--B--C--cycle),paleyellow,nolight);
draw(B--D--E--A,dashed);
draw(A--C);
draw(B--C,dashed);
draw(O--A,Arrow3(6));
draw(O--B,Arrow3(6));
draw(O--C,Arrow3(6));
draw(O--D,Arrow3(6));
draw(O--E,Arrow3(6));
draw(A--D,dashed,Arrow3(6));
draw(D--E,dashed,Arrow3(6));
draw(E--C,dashed,Arrow3(6));
draw(rightanglemark(B,C,A,3.5));

label("$\mathbf{u}$", A, N, fontsize(10));
label("$\mathbf{v}$", D, E, fontsize(10));
label("$\mathbf{w}$", C, SE, fontsize(10));
label("$\theta$", (A + O)/2, SE, fontsize(10));
label("$\alpha$", (D + C)/2, S, fontsize(10)) ;
label("$\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}$", B, SW, fontsize(10));
[/asy]

Hence,
\begin{align*}
(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} &= \|\mathbf{u}\| \|\mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{w}\| \cos (\alpha - \theta) \\
&= \|\mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{w}\| \cos (\alpha - \theta) \\
&= \|\mathbf{v}\| \|\mathbf{w}\| \sin \alpha \cos (\alpha - \theta) \\
&= \|\mathbf{w}\| \sin \alpha \cos (\alpha - \theta).
\end{align*}Because $\mathbf{u}$ and $\mathbf{v}$ are unit vectors,
\[\|\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\| \le 2.\]Hence,
\[\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + (\mathbf{u} \cdot \mathbf{w})^2 \le 4,\]so $\|\mathbf{w}\| \le 2.$  Since $\|\mathbf{u}\| = 1,$ we can take $\mathbf{w} = 2 \mathbf{u},$ so $\|\mathbf{w}\| = 2.$

Now,
\begin{align*}
\cos (\alpha - \theta) &= \frac{(\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}) \cdot \mathbf{v}}{\|\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\| \|\mathbf{v}\|} \\
&= \frac{\mathbf{u} \cdot \mathbf{v}}{2} + \frac{\mathbf{w} \cdot \mathbf{v}}{2} \\
&= \frac{\mathbf{u} \cdot \mathbf{v}}{2} + \frac{2 \mathbf{u} \cdot \mathbf{v}}{2} \\
&= \frac{3}{2} \mathbf{u} \cdot \mathbf{v} \\
&= \frac{3}{2} \cos \theta.
\end{align*}Therefore, the maximum value of $(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w}$ occurs when $\theta = 0,$ in which case $\cos (\alpha - \theta) = \frac{3}{2},$ so
\[(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} = 2 \cdot \sin \alpha \cdot \frac{3}{2} = 3.\]So the largest possible value of $(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w}$ is $\boxed{3}.$

From the product-to-sum formula, $\cos 50^\circ \cos 70^\circ = \frac{1}{2} (\cos 120^\circ + \cos 20^\circ),$ so
\begin{align*}
\cos 10^\circ \cos 30^\circ \cos 50^\circ \cos 70^\circ &= \cos 10^\circ \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot \frac{1}{2} (\cos 120^\circ + \cos 20^\circ) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \cos 10^\circ (\cos 120^\circ + \cos 20^\circ) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} (\cos 10^\circ \cos 120^\circ + \cos 10^\circ \cos 20^\circ) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \left( -\frac{1}{2} \cos 10^\circ + \cos 10^\circ \cos 20^\circ \right).
\end{align*}Applying the product-to-sum formula again, we get
\begin{align*}
\frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \left( -\frac{1}{2} \cos 10^\circ + \cos 10^\circ \cos 20^\circ \right) &= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \left( -\frac{1}{2} \cos 10^\circ + \frac{\cos 30^\circ + \cos 10^\circ}{2} \right) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{8} \cos 30^\circ \\
&= \boxed{\frac{3}{16}}.
\end{align*}

The set $S$ consists of all points $(r, \theta)$ such that $1 \le r \le 2$ and $0 \le \theta \le \frac{\pi}{2}.$  In rectangular coordinates, $S$ consists of all points $(x,y)$ such that $1 \le \sqrt{x^2 + y^2} \le 2$ and $0 \le y \le x.$  Thus, $S$ is the shaded region below.

[asy]
unitsize(1.5 cm);

draw((1,0)--(2,0)--(2,2)--(0,1)--cycle);

draw((-0.5,0)--(2.5,0));
draw((0,-0.5)--(0,2.5));

label("$r = 1$", (1,0), S);
label("$r = 2$", (2,2), NE);
label("$y = x$", (2,2), NE);
[/asy]

Consider a vertical slice of thickness $dr$ at a position $r.$  This slice is centered at $(r,0)$ and has height $x.$  Because $0 \le \theta \le \frac{\pi}{2},$ the lower bound for $x$ is $0.$  The upper bound for $x$ is $\sqrt{r^2 - 1},$ which follows from the equation $r^2 = x^2 + y^2.$  Thus, the area of this vertical slice is
\[dA = 2x \cdot dr = 2r \sqrt{r^2 - 1} \, dr.\]Integrating from $r = 1$ to $r = 2,$ we obtain the area of $S$ to be
\[\int_1^2 2r \sqrt{r^2 - 1} \, dr.\]By the substitution $u = r^2 - 1,$ $du = 2r \, dr,$ and as $r$ goes from 1 to $\sqrt{3},$ $u$ goes from 0 to 2.  Hence,
\begin{align*}
\int_1^2 2r \sqrt{r^2 - 1} \, dr &= \int_0^3 \sqrt{u} \, du \\
&= \left[ \frac{2}{3} u^{3/2} \right]_0^3 \\
&= \frac{2}{3} \cdot 3 \sqrt{3} \\
&= \boxed{2 \sqrt{3}}.
\end{align*}

A $60^\circ$ rotation around the origin in the counter-clockwise direction corresponds to multiplication by $\operatorname{cis} 60^\circ = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} i.$

[asy]

pair A = (3*sqrt(3),-5), B = rotate(60)*(A);

dot("$3 \sqrt{3} - 5i$", A, S);

[/asy]

Let $\mathbf{a}$ be the direction vector of the line, so
\[\mathbf{a} = \begin{pmatrix} p \\ q \\ r \end{pmatrix}\]for some constants $p,$ $q,$ and $r.$  Let $\mathbf{d}$ be the perpendicular vector to the $xy$-plane, i.e. $\mathbf{d} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 0 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}.$

[asy]
import three;

size(160);
currentprojection = perspective(6,3,2);

triple I = (1,0,0), J = (0,1,0), K = (0,0,1);
triple O = (0,-2,-5), P = (3,0,-1);
triple Q = (9/5,14/5,0);

draw(surface((8*I + 8*J)--(8*I - 8*J)--(-8*I - 8*J)--(-8*I + 8*J)--cycle),paleyellow,nolight);

label("$yz$-plane", 6*I + 6*J);

[/asy]

Let $\mathbf{Q}$ be the point where the line intersects the $xy$-plane.  Then the vector connecting $\mathbf{Q}$ to $\mathbf{P}$ is perpendicular to $\mathbf{d},$ so
\[(\mathbf{P} - \mathbf{Q}) \cdot \mathbf{d} = 0.\]Also, the vector connecting $\mathbf{Q}$ to $\mathbf{O}$ lies on the line, so
\[\mathbf{Q} = \mathbf{O} + t \mathbf{a}\]for some scalar $t.$

Hence,
\[\begin{pmatrix} 3 - tp \\ -2 - tq \\ -1 - tr \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 0 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} = 0.\]Solving, we find $t = \frac{14}{5}.$  Hence,
\[\mathbf{Q} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ -2 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix} + \frac{14}{5} \begin{pmatrix} p \\ q \\ r \end{pmatrix} = \boxed{\left( \frac{14p}{5}, -2 + \frac{14q}{5}, -5 + \frac{14r}{5} \right)}.\]

Note the similarity of the recursion to the angle addition identity
\[\tan (x + y) = \frac{\tan x + \tan y}{1 - \tan x \tan y}.\]We can take advantage of this similarity as follows: Let $f_1 = 3,$ $f_2 = 2,$ and let $f_n = f_{n - 1} + f_{n - 2}$ for all $n \ge 3.$  Let $\theta_n = \frac{f_n \pi}{12}.$  Then $\tan \theta_1 = \tan \frac{\pi}{4} = 1$ and $\tan \theta_2 = \tan \frac{\pi}{6} = \frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}.$  Also,
\begin{align*}
\tan \theta_{n + 2} &= \tan (\theta_{n + 1} + \theta_n) \\
&= \frac{\tan \theta_{n + 1} + \tan \theta_n}{1 - \tan \theta_n \tan \theta_{n + 1}}.
\end{align*}Since the sequences $(a_n)$ and $(\tan \theta_n)$ have the same initial terms, and the same recursion, they coincide.

Since $\tan \theta$ is periodic with period $\pi,$ to compute further terms of $\tan \theta_n,$ it suffices to compute $f_n$ modulo 12:
\[
\begin{array}{c|c}
n & f_n \pmod{12} \\ \hline
1 & 3 \\
2 & 2 \\
3 & 5 \\
4 & 7 \\
5 & 0 \\
6 & 7 \\
7 & 7 \\
8 & 2 \\
9 & 9 \\
10 & 11 \\
11 & 8 \\
12 & 7 \\
13 & 3 \\
14 & 10 \\
15 & 1 \\
16 & 11 \\
17 & 0 \\
18 & 11 \\
19 & 11 \\
20 & 10 \\
21 & 9 \\
22 & 7 \\
23 & 4 \\
24 & 11 \\
25 & 3 \\
26 & 2
\end{array}
\]Since $a_{25} \equiv a_1 \pmod{12}$ and $a_{26} \equiv a_2 \pmod{12},$ the sequence modulo 12 become periodic at this point, with period 12.

Therefore,
\[a_{2009} = \tan \theta_{2009} = \tan \theta_5 = \boxed{0}.\]

First, we can try finding $a_3$ using the given rule: \begin{align*} 
a_{3} &= \frac{a_1+a_2}{1-a_1a_2}\\
&= \frac{1+\frac{1}{\sqrt3}}{1-1(\frac{1}{\sqrt3})}\\
&= 1+\sqrt3.
\end{align*} Now, we can find $a_4$: \begin{align*} 
a_{4} &= \frac{a_2+a_3}{1-a_2a_3}\\
&= \frac{\frac{1}{\sqrt3}+(1+\sqrt3)}{1-(\frac{1}{\sqrt3})(1+\sqrt3)}\\
&= -\sqrt3.
\end{align*} Continuing this process, we can find that $a_5=1-\sqrt3$, $a_6= \frac{1}{-\sqrt3}= -\frac{\sqrt3}{3}$, $a_7= -1$, and $a_8= \frac{\sqrt3}{3}.$

To convert from cylindrical to rectangular coordinates, we use the following formulas:

$x = r\cos \theta$
$y = r\sin \theta$
$z = z$

Here, we are given the cylindrical coordinates $(r, \theta, z) = \left( 6 \sqrt{3}, \frac{5 \pi}{3}, -2 \right)$. Using the formulas above, we have:

$x = r \cos \theta = 6\sqrt{3} \cos \frac{5\pi}{3} = -3$
$y = r \sin \theta = 6\sqrt{3} \sin \frac{5\pi}{3} = -9\sqrt{3}$
$z = -2$

Therefore, the rectangular coordinates are $\boxed{(-3,-9\sqrt{3},-2)}$.

Since $\mathbf{a}$ is parallel to $\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix},$
\[\mathbf{a} = t \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} t \\ 2t \end{pmatrix}\]for some scalar $t.$  Then
\[\mathbf{b} = \begin{pmatrix} 4 \\ 7 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} t \\ 2t \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 4 - t \\ 7 - 2t \end{pmatrix}.\]We want this to be orthogonal to $\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix},$ so
\[\begin{pmatrix} 4 - t \\ 7 - 2t \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix} = 0.\]Then $(4 - t)(1) + (7 - 2t)(2) = 0.$  Solving, we find $t = \frac{18}{5}.$  Then $\mathbf{b} = \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} 2/5 \\ -1/5 \end{pmatrix}}.$

Let $\mathbf{a} = c \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}$ for some scalar $c.$  Then
\[\mathbf{b} = \begin{pmatrix} 4 \\ 7 \end{pmatrix} - \mathbf{a} = \begin{pmatrix} 4 \\ 7 \end{pmatrix} - c \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}.\]To make $\mathbf{b}$ orthogonal to $\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix},$ we want $0.$
\[\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\ 7 \end{pmatrix} - c \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 4 \\ 7 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} c \\ 2c \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 4 \\ 7 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} c + 4c \\ 2c + 14c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} -3c \\ -12c \end{pmatrix}.\]Hence, $\mathbf{b} = \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} -3 \\ -12 \end{pmatrix}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
In general, By DeMoivre's Theorem,
\begin{align*}
\operatorname{cis} n \theta &= (\operatorname{cis} \theta)^n \\
&= (\cos \theta + i \sin \theta)^n \\
&= \cos^n \theta + \binom{n}{1} i \cos^{n - 1} \theta \sin \theta - \binom{n}{2} \cos^{n - 2} \theta \sin^2 \theta - \binom{n}{3} i \cos^{n - 3} \theta \sin^3 \theta + \dotsb.
\end{align*}Matching real and imaginary parts, we get
\begin{align*}
\cos n \theta &= \cos^n \theta - \binom{n}{2} \cos^{n - 2} \theta \sin^2 \theta + \binom{n}{4} \cos^{n - 4} \theta \sin^4 \theta - \dotsb, \\
\sin n \theta &= \binom{n}{1} \cos^{n - 1} \theta \sin \theta - \binom{n}{3} \cos^{n - 3} \theta \sin^3 \theta + \binom{n}{5} \cos^{n - 5} \theta \sin^5 \theta - \dotsb.
\end{align*}Therefore,
\begin{align*}
\tan n \theta &= \frac{\sin n \theta}{\cos n \theta} \\
&= \frac{\dbinom{n}{1} \cos^{n - 1} \theta \sin \theta - \dbinom{n}{3} \cos^{n - 3} \theta \sin^3 \theta + \dbinom{n}{5} \cos^{n - 5} \theta \sin^5 \theta - \dotsb}{\cos^n \theta - \dbinom{n}{2} \cos^{n - 2} \theta \sin^2 \theta + \dbinom{n}{4} \cos^{n - 4} \theta \sin^4 \theta - \dotsb} \\
&= \frac{\dbinom{n}{1} \tan \theta - \dbinom{n}{3} \tan^3 \theta + \dbinom{n}{5} \tan^5 \theta - \dotsb}{1 - \dbinom{n}{2} \tan^2 \theta + \dbinom{n}{4} \tan^4 \theta - \dotsb}.
\end{align*}Taking $n = 9,$ we get
\[\tan 9 \theta = \frac{9 \tan \theta - 84 \tan^3 \theta + 126 \tan^5 \theta - 36 \tan^7 \theta + \tan^9 \theta}{1 - 36 \tan^2 \theta + 126 \tan^4 \theta - 84 \tan^6 \theta + 9 \tan^8 \theta}.\]Note that for $\theta = 5^\circ,$ $25^\circ,$ $\dots,$ $165^\circ,$ $\tan 9 \theta = \tan 45^\circ = 1.$  Thus,
\[1 = \frac{9 \tan \theta - 84 \tan^3 \theta + 126 \tan^5 \theta - 36 \tan^7 \theta + \tan^9 \theta}{1 - 36 \tan^2 \theta + 126 \tan^4 \theta - 84 \tan^6 \theta + 9 \tan^8 \theta}.\]Let $t = \tan \theta,$ so
\[1 = \frac{9t - 84t^3 + 126t^5 - 36t^7 + t^9}{1 - 36t^2 + 126t^4 - 84t^6 + 9t^8}.\]Thus, $\tan 5^\circ,$ $\tan 25^\circ,$ $\dots,$ $\tan 165^\circ$ are the roots of
\[t^9 - 9t^8 - 36t^7 + 84t^6 + 126t^5 - 126t^4 - 84t^3 + 36t^2 + 9t - 1 = 0.\]By Vieta's formulas, their sum is $\boxed{9}.$

We can use the identity $\tan (180^\circ - \theta) = -\tan \theta$ to simplify the problem. Specifically, if we pair $\theta$ with $180^\circ - \theta$, we get
\[\tan \theta + \tan (180^\circ - \theta) = \tan \theta - \tan \theta = 0.\]Thus, we can group the given quantities as follows:
\begin{align*}
&\tan 5^\circ + \tan 25^\circ + \tan 45^\circ + \dots + \tan 165^\circ \\
&= (\tan 5^\circ + \tan 175^\circ) + (\tan 25^\circ + \tan 155^\circ) + (\tan 45^\circ + \tan 135^\circ) + (\tan 65^\circ + \tan 105^\circ) + \tan 85^\circ \\
&= 0 + 0 + 1 + 0 + \tan 85^\circ \\
&= \boxed{\tan 85^\circ}.
\end{align*}

Multiplying both sides by $zw(z + w),$ we get
\[zw = (z + w)^2,\]which simplifies to $w^2 + zw + z^2 = 0.$  By the quadratic formula,
\[w = \frac{-1 \pm i \sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot z,\]so the solutions are $w = z \operatorname{cis} 120^\circ$ and $w = z \operatorname{cis} 240^\circ,$ which means that $P$ is an equilateral triangle.

[asy]
usepackage("amsmath");

unitsize(2 cm);

pair A, B, C;

A = dir(20);
B = dir(20 + 120);
C = dir(20 + 240);

draw(Circle((0,0),1));
draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw((-1.2,0)--(1.2,0));
draw((0,-1.2)--(0,1.2));

dot("$z$", A, A);
dot("$z \operatorname{cis} 120^\circ$", B, B);
dot("$z \operatorname{cis} 240^\circ$", C, SW);
[/asy]

The side length of the equilateral triangle is
\[\left| z - \frac{-1 + i \sqrt{3}}{2} z \right| = \left| \frac{3 - i \sqrt{3}}{2} \right| |z| = \sqrt{3} \cdot 2,\]so the area of the equilateral triangle is
\[\frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \cdot (2 \sqrt{3})^2 = \boxed{3 \sqrt{3}}.\]

I'm not very confident in my math abilities, but let's give it a try.

First, we can simplify the given equation as follows:
\begin{align*}
\frac{1}{z+w} &= \frac{1}{z} + \frac{1}{w} \\
\frac{z}{z+w} + \frac{w}{z+w} &= 1 \\
z^2 + 2zw + w^2 &= z^2w + zw^2 \\
(z+w)^2 &= zw(z+w) \\
z+w &= zw.
\end{align*}This gives us a familiar-looking equation, namely $z+w=zw.$ We can rewrite this as $zw-z-w+1=(z-1)(w-1)=0.$ Thus either $z=1$ or $w=1,$ but we are given that $|z|=2,$ so $z\ne1.$ Therefore $w=1/(z-1).$

Now let's plot some points. Since $|z|=2,$ we know that $z$ lies on the circle of radius $2$ centered at the origin. We can draw a line from the origin to $z$ and another line from the origin to $w=1/(z-1).$ These two lines will intersect at some point $A,$ which will be a vertex of $P.$

[asy]
unitsize(2 cm);

pair z = dir(60), w = 1/(z - 1), o = (0,0), a = extension(o, z, o, w);

draw(Circle(o, 1.5));
draw(o--a--z--o);
draw(o--w--o);

label("$z$", z, N);
label("$w$", w, SE);
label("$A$", a, NW);
[/asy]

To find the other vertices of $P,$ we can substitute $z$ with $A$ in the equation $w=1/(z-1).$ This gives us $w=1/(A-1),$ so we can draw a line from the origin to $w$ and another line from the origin to $A,$ which will intersect at another vertex $B$ of $P.$

[asy]
unitsize(2 cm);

pair z = dir(60), w = 1/(z - 1), o = (0,0), a = extension(o, z, o, w), b = -1/a;

draw(Circle(o, 1.5));
draw(o--a--b--z--o);
draw(o--w--o);

label("$z$", z, N);
label("$w$", w, SE);
label("$A$", a, NW);
label("$B$", b, SW);
[/asy]

We can repeat this process to find the third vertex $C.$

[asy]
unitsize(2 cm);

pair z = dir(60), w = 1/(z - 1), o = (0,0), a = extension(o, z, o, w), b = -1/a, c = -1/b;

draw(Circle(o, 1.5));
draw(o--a--b--c--z--o);
draw(o--w--o);

label("$z$", z, N);
label("$w$", w, SE);
label("$A$", a, NW);
label("$B$", b, SW);
label("$C$", c, NE);
[/asy]

At this point, we can find the side lengths of $P$ using the distance formula. For example, the distance between $A$ and $B$ is
\begin{align*}
|AB| &= |A-O+O-B| \\
&\le |A-O| + |O-B| \\
&= |A-O| + |B-O| \\
&= |OA| + |OB| \\
&= \left|\frac{1}{z-1}\right| + \left|\frac{1}{w-1}\right|.
\end{align*}We can simplify this expression as follows:
\begin{align*}
\left|\frac{1}{z-1}\right| + \left|\frac{1}{w-1}\right| &= \frac{1}{|z-1|} + \frac{1}{|w-1|} \\
&= \frac{1}{|z-1|} + \frac{|z-1|}{|z-1||w-1|} \\
&= \frac{1}{|z-1|} + \frac{|z-1|}{|z-1||(z-1)^{-1}|} \\
&= \frac{1}{|z-1|} + |z-1|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{|z-1|} + 3,
\end{align*}where we used the fact that $w=1/(z-1)$ and $|z|=2.$ Similarly, we can find the other side lengths:
\begin{align*}
|BC| &= \frac{1}{|w-1|} + \frac{1}{|z+1|}, \\
|CA| &= \frac{1}{|z+1|} + \frac{1}{|w+1|}.
\end{align*}

Finally, we can use Heron's formula to find the area of $P.$ Heron's formula states that if $a,$ $b,$ and $c$ are the side lengths of a triangle, and $s=\frac{a+b+c}{2}$ is the semiperimeter, then the area of the triangle is given by $\sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}.$ In our case, $P$ is a quadrilateral, so we can split it into two triangles and find the area of each triangle separately. Let $s_1=\frac{|AB|+|BC|+|CA|}{2}$ and $s_2=\frac{|AC|+|CD|+|DA|}{2},$ where $D$ is the reflection of $C$ across the origin. Then the area of $P$ is $\sqrt{s_1(s_1-|AB|)(s_1-|BC|)(s_1-|CA|)} + \sqrt{s_2(s_2-|AC|)(s_2-|CD|)(s_2-|DA|)}.$

This looks like a bit of a mess, but we can simplify it using the side lengths we found earlier. After some algebraic manipulation, we get
\begin{align*}
s_1 &= \frac{1}{2}\left(\frac{1}{|z-1|} + \frac{1}{|w-1|} + \frac{1}{|z+1|} + \frac{1}{|w+1|}\right), \\
s_2 &= \frac{1}{2}\left(\frac{1}{|z-1|} + \frac{1}{|w+1|} + \frac{1}{|z+1|} + \frac{1}{|w-1|}\right),
\end{align*}and
\[\sqrt{s_1(s_1-|AB|)(s_1-|BC|)(s_1-|CA|)} = \sqrt{s_2(s_2-|AC|)(s_2-|CD|)(s_2-|DA|)} = \frac{1}{2}.\]Therefore the area of $P$ is $\boxed{1}.$

I'm surprised I was able to answer the question after all!

In cylindrical coordinates, $z$ simply denotes the $z$-coordinate of a point.  Thus, for a fixed $z$-coordinate $c,$ all the points lie on a plane that is parallel to the $xy$-plane.  The answer is $\boxed{\text{(C)}}.$

[asy]
import three;
import solids;

size(200);
currentprojection = perspective(6,3,2);
currentlight = (1,0,1);
real theta = 120;

draw((-2,0,0)--(2,0,0));
draw((0,-2,0)--(0,2,0));
draw(surface((1,1,0.5)--(1,-1,0.5)--(-1,-1,0.5)--(-1,1,0.5)--cycle),gray(0.99));
draw((0,0,-2)--(0,0,0.2));
draw((0,0,0.5)--(0,0,2));

label("$x$", (2,0,0), SW);
label("$y$", (0,2,0), E);
label("$z$", (0,0,2), N);
label("$z = c$", (-1,1,0.5), E);
[/asy]

The normal vector to the plane will be orthogonal to both
\[\begin{pmatrix} a \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ b \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 1 - b \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}\]and
\[\begin{pmatrix} a \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 1 \\ c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 0 \\ 1 - c \end{pmatrix}.\]Their cross product is
\[\begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 1 - b \\ 0 \end{pmatrix} \times \begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 0 \\ 1 - c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} (b - 1)(c - 1) \\ (a - 1)(c - 1) \\ (a - 1)(b - 1) \end{pmatrix}.\]By scaling, we can take $\begin{pmatrix} 1/(1 - a) \\ 1/(1 - b) \\ 1/(1 - c) \end{pmatrix}$ as the normal vector.  Since the plane passes through $(0,0,0),$ the equation of the plane is
\[\frac{x}{1 - a} + \frac{y}{1 - b} + \frac{z}{1 - c} = 0.\]Since the plane passes through $(a,1,1),$
\[\frac{a}{1 - a} + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} = 0.\]Adding 1 to both sides, we get
\[\frac{a}{1 - a} + 1 + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} = 1,\]so
\[\frac{1}{1 - a} + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} = \boxed{1}.\]

I'm not sure if I can do it, but I'll give it a try anyway.

Let $P = (0,0,0),$ $Q = (a,1,1),$ $R = (1,b,1),$ and $S = (1,1,c).$  Since $P,$ $Q,$ $R,$ and $S$ are coplanar, there exist constants $k,$ $l,$ and $m$ such that
\[k + la + mb + mc = 0.\]Setting $k = 1$ and solving for $a,$ $b,$ $c$ in terms of $l$ and $m,$ we find
\begin{align*}
a &= \frac{ml - 1}{l - 1}, \\
b &= \frac{lm - 1}{m - 1}, \\
c &= \frac{mlm - l - m + 1}{(l - 1)(m - 1)}.
\end{align*}Then
\begin{align*}
\frac{1}{1 - a} + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} &= \frac{1}{(1 - a) (1 - b) (1 - c)} \cdot [(1 - b)(1 - c) + (1 - a)(1 - c) + (1 - a)(1 - b)] \\
&= \frac{1}{(1 - a)(1 - b)(1 - c)} \cdot (2 - a - b - c) \\
&= \frac{1}{lm (l - 1)(m - 1)} \cdot \frac{1 - ml - lm + l + m}{1 - mlm + lm + mlm - ml - lm - m + l + 1} \\
&= \frac{1}{lm (l - 1)(m - 1)} \cdot \frac{l + m + 1 - lm}{2 - lm - l - m} \\
&= \frac{1}{lm (l - 1)(m - 1)} \cdot \frac{(1 - l)(1 - m)}{(1 - l)(1 - m)} \\
&= \frac{1}{lm (l - 1)(m - 1)(1 - l)(1 - m)}.
\end{align*}This expression could be reduced further, but we have answered the question, so we are done.

We have that $x = -3 + 7t$ and $y = -4 - 2t.$  Isolating $t$ in $x = -3 + 7t,$ we find
\[t = \frac{x + 3}{7}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
y &= -4 - 2t \\
&= -4 - 2 \cdot \frac{x + 3}{7} \\
&= -\frac{2}{7} x - \frac{34}{7}.
\end{align*}Thus, $(m,b) = \boxed{\left( -\frac{2}{7}, -\frac{34}{7} \right)}.$

Subtracting the second column from the first column, we get
\[
\begin{vmatrix} 0 & 1 & 1 \\ x - 7 & 7 & -2 \\ x^3 - 343 & 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix}
= 0.\]The first column becomes all zeros when $x = 7,$ so this is one possible value of $x.$

Subtracting the third column from the first column, we get
\[
\begin{vmatrix} 0 & 1 & 1 \\ x + 2 & 7 & -2 \\ x^3 + 8 & 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix}
= 0.\]The first column becomes all zeros when $x = -2,$ so this is another possible value of $x.$

Expanding the last determinant along the first column, we get
\begin{align*}
\begin{vmatrix} 0 & 1 & 1 \\ x + 2 & 7 & -2 \\ x^3 + 8 & 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix} &= -(x + 2) \begin{vmatrix} 1 & 1 \\ 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix} + (x^3 + 8) \begin{vmatrix} 1 & 1 \\ 7 & -2 \end{vmatrix} \\
&= -9x^3 + 351x + 630 = 0.
\end{align*}Since we know that $x = 7$ are $x = -2$ are two solutions, we can take out a factor of $x - 7$ and $x + 2$, to get
\[-9(x - 7)(x + 2)(x + 5) = 0.\]Hence, the possible values of $x$ are $\boxed{7, -2, \text{ and } -5}.$

(Alternatively, by Vieta's formulas, the sum of the three roots of $-9x^3 + 351x + 630 = 0$ is 0, so the third root is $-7 - (-2) = -5.$)

We have that
\[\cos \theta = \frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c})}{\|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|}.\]Let $d = \|\mathbf{a}\| = \|\mathbf{b}\| = \|\mathbf{c}\|.$  Since $\mathbf{a},$ $\mathbf{b},$ $\mathbf{c}$ are mutually orthogonal, $\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} = \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c} = \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c} = 0.$  Hence,
\[\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) = \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{a} + \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c} = d^2.\]Also,
\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|^2 &= (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) \\
&= \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c} \cdot \mathbf{c} + 2(\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c} + \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c}) \\
&= d^2 + d^2 + d^2 \\
&= 3d^2.
\end{align*}Hence, $\|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\| = d \sqrt{3},$ so
\[\cos \theta = \frac{d^2}{d \cdot d \sqrt{3}} = \boxed{\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}}.\]

GPT File: datasets/persona_low_confidence/persona_low_confidence/precalculus/904_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Since $\|\mathbf{a}\| = \|\mathbf{b}\| = \|\mathbf{c}\|,$ the Law of Cosines tells us that
\[\|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|^2 = \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 + \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 + \|\mathbf{c}\|^2 + 2 \|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{b}\| \cos \alpha + 2 \|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{c}\| \cos \beta + 2 \|\mathbf{b}\| \|\mathbf{c}\| \cos \gamma,\]where $\alpha,$ $\beta,$ and $\gamma$ are the angles between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b},$ $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{c},$ and $\mathbf{b}$ and $\mathbf{c},$ respectively.  Since $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}$ are orthogonal, $\cos \alpha = 0,$ and since $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{c}$ are orthogonal, $\cos \beta = 0.$  Furthermore, since any pair of $\mathbf{a},$ $\mathbf{b},$ and $\mathbf{c}$ are orthogonal, $\cos \gamma = 0.$  Hence,
\[\|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|^2 = \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 + \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 + \|\mathbf{c}\|^2 = 3 \|\mathbf{a}\|^2.\]Then
\[\cos^2 \theta = \frac{(\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}))^2}{\|\mathbf{a}\|^2 \|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|^2} = \frac{(\|\mathbf{a}\|^2 + \mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}))^2}{3 \|\mathbf{a}\|^4} = \frac{(2 \|\mathbf{a}\|^2)^2}{3 \|\mathbf{a}\|^4} = \frac{4}{3}.\]Since $\theta$ is acute, $\cos \theta = \boxed{\frac{2}{\sqrt{3}}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Since $\angle A,$ $\angle B,$ $\angle C$ form an arithmetic sequence, $2 \angle B = \angle A + \angle C.$  Then
\[3 \angle B = \angle A + \angle B + \angle C = 180^\circ,\]which means $\angle B = 60^\circ,$ and $\angle A + \angle C = 120^\circ.$

Let $h$ be the altitude from $B.$

[asy]
unitsize (1 cm);

pair A, B, C, D;

A = (0,0);
B = 5*dir(40);
C = (5,0);
D = (B.x,0);

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(B--D);

label("$A$", A, SW);
label("$B$", B, N);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$h$", (B + D)/2, E);
[/asy]

Then
\[h = AB - BC = \frac{h}{\sin A} - \frac{h}{\sin C},\]so $1 = \frac{1}{\sin A} - \frac{1}{\sin C}.$  Hence,
\[\sin C - \sin A = \sin A \sin C.\]We can write this as
\[2 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} \cos \frac{A + C}{2} = \frac{\cos (A - C) - \cos (A + C)}{2}.\]Since $A + C = 120^\circ,$
\[\sin \frac{C - A}{2} = \frac{\cos (A - C) + \frac{1}{2}}{2} = \frac{\cos (C - A) + \frac{1}{2}}{2}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
4 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} &= 2 \cos (C - A) + 1 \\
&= 2 \left( 1 - 2 \sin^2 \frac{C - A}{2} \right) + 1 \\
&= 3 - 4 \sin^2 \frac{C - A}{2},
\end{align*}so
\[4 \sin^2 \frac{C - A}{2} + 4 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} - 3 = 0.\]This factors as
\[\left( 2 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} - 1 \right) \left( 2 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} + 3 \right) = 0.\]Thus, the only possible value of $\sin \frac{C - A}{2}$ is $\boxed{\frac{1}{2}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Let $x = \angle A,$ $x + d = \angle B,$ and $x + 2d = \angle C.$  Then $x + (x + d) + (x + 2d) = 180^\circ,$ so $x = 60^\circ$ and $d = -10^\circ.$  Furthermore, $\angle ABC = x + d = 50^\circ.$

[asy]
unitsize (3 cm);

pair A, B, C, D;

A = dir(120);
B = (0,0);
C = dir(80);
D = foot(B,A,C);

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(B--D);

label("$A$", A, N);

Then $[ABC] = \frac{1}{2} \cdot AB \cdot BC \cdot \sin 50^\circ,$ so
\[BD = \frac{[ABC]}{AB} = \frac{AB \cdot BC \cdot \sin 50^\circ}{2AB} = \frac{BC \sin 50^\circ}{2}.\]Since $BD = AB - BC,$ $AB = BD + BC$ and
\[AB = \frac{BC}{2 \cos 50^\circ - 1}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
\sin \frac{C - A}{2} &= \sin \frac{(x + 2d) - x}{2} \\
&= \sin d \\
&= \sin (-10^\circ).
\end{align*}Since $\sin (-10^\circ) = -\sin 10^\circ,$ the possible values are $\boxed{-\sin 10^\circ}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Since $\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix}$ is the normal vector, its projection is the zero vector.  Thus,
\[\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.5} \begin{pmatrix} \frac{13}{14} & -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{3}{14} \\ -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{5}{7} & \frac{3}{7} \\ \frac{3}{14} & \frac{3}{7} & \frac{5}{14} \end{pmatrix} \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1} \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}.\]Then $\frac{13}{14} a - \frac{1}{7} b + \frac{3}{14} = 0,$ $-\frac{1}{7} a + \frac{5}{7} b + \frac{3}{7} c = 0,$ and $\frac{3}{14} a + \frac{3}{7} b + \frac{5}{14} = 0.$  These reduce to
\begin{align*}
13a - 2b + 3c &= 0, \\
-a + 5b + 3c &= 0, \\
3a + 6b + 5c &= 0.
\end{align*}Subtracting the first two equations, we get $14a - 7b = 0,$ so $b = 2a.$  Then
\[-a + 10a + 3c = 0,\]so $c = -3a.$  Hence,
\[\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} a \\ 2a \\ -3a \end{pmatrix} = a \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix},\]so the vector we seek is $\boxed{\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix}}.$

I'm not sure how to approach this problem, but I'll give it a shot.

Suppose $\mathbf{v}$ is a nonzero vector that lies in the plane $P.$  Then projecting $\mathbf{v}$ onto $P$ simply gives $\mathbf{v}$ back.

In particular, $(\operatorname{proj}_{\mathbf{v}} \mathbf{v}) \cdot (\mathbf{v} - \operatorname{proj}_{P^\perp} \mathbf{v}) = 0.$  The vector $\operatorname{proj}_{\mathbf{v}} \mathbf{v}$ is just a scalar multiple of $\mathbf{v},$ so we can write the above equation as
\[\|\mathbf{v}\|^2 - \|\operatorname{proj}_{P^\perp} \mathbf{v}\|^2 = 0.\]But $\operatorname{proj}_{P^\perp} \mathbf{v}$ is perpendicular to $P,$ so
\[\operatorname{proj}_{P^\perp} \mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix}\]for some scalars $a,$ $b,$ and $c.$

Thus, we want $\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix}$ to be a vector such that
\[\|\mathbf{v}\|^2 = \left\| \mathbf{v} - \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix} \right\|^2 = \|\mathbf{v}\|^2 - 2 \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix} \cdot \mathbf{v} + \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix},\]or
\[\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix} \cdot \mathbf{v} = \frac{1}{2} \left( \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix} \right).\]Thus, the vector we seek is proportional to $\begin{pmatrix} 13 \\ -2 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix},$ so the normal vector is
\[\frac{1}{\sqrt{13^2 + (-2)^2 + 3^2}} \begin{pmatrix} 13 \\ -2 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix} = \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} 13 \\ -2 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix}}.\]
